A musket of several centuries ago is still similar in principle to a modern-day rifle—using the expansion of gases to propel projectiles over long distances—albeit less accurately and rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_models">Early models</span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Fire_lances">Fire lances</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fire_lance" title="Fire lance">Fire lance</a></div>
<p>The Chinese <a href="/wiki/Fire_lance" title="Fire lance">fire lance</a> from the 10th century was the direct predecessor to the modern concept of the firearm. It was not a gun itself, but an addition to soldiers' spears. Originally it consisted of paper or bamboo barrels that would contain incendiary gunpowder that could be lit one time and which would project flames at the enemy. Sometimes Chinese troops would place small projectiles within the barrel that would also be projected when the gunpowder was lit, but most of the explosive force would create flames. Later, the barrel was changed to be made of metal, so that more explosive gunpowder could be used and put more force into the propulsion of projectiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chase_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chase-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 31–32">: 31–32 </span></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Hand_cannons">Hand cannons</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hand_cannon" title="Hand cannon">Hand cannon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lgehumble_1400.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="1333" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Lgehumble_1400.jpg/220px-Lgehumble_1400.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Lgehumble_1400.jpg/330px-Lgehumble_1400.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Lgehumble_1400.jpg/440px-Lgehumble_1400.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lgehumble_1400.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hand_cannon" title="Hand cannon">Hand cannon</a> being fired from a stand, "Belli Fortis", manuscript, by Konrad Kyeser, 1400</div></div></div>
<p>The original predecessors of all firearms, the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Fire_lance" title="Fire lance">fire lance</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2018)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> and <a href="/wiki/Hand_cannon" title="Hand cannon">hand cannon</a>, were loaded with gunpowder and the shot (initially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead_shot" title="Lead shot">lead shot</a>, later replaced by cast iron<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>) through the muzzle, while a fuse was placed at the rear. This fuse was lit, causing the gunpowder to ignite and propel the projectiles. In military use, the standard hand cannon was tremendously powerful, while also being somewhat erratic<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> due to the relative inability of the gunner to aim the weapon, or to control the ballistic properties of the projectile. Recoil could be absorbed by bracing the barrel against the ground using a wooden support, the forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/Stock_(firearms)" title="Stock (firearms)">stock</a>. Neither the quality nor amount of gunpowder, nor the consistency in projectile dimensions was controlled, with resulting inaccuracy in firing due to <a href="/wiki/Windage" title="Windage">windage</a>, variance in gunpowder composition, and the difference in diameter between the <a href="/wiki/Caliber" title="Caliber">bore</a> and the shot. Hand cannons were replaced<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (February 2022)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> by lighter carriage-mounted <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> pieces, and ultimately by the <a href="/wiki/Arquebus" title="Arquebus">arquebus</a>.
</p><p>In the 1420s gunpowder was used to propel missiles from hand-held tubes during <a href="/wiki/History_of_Czech_civilian_firearms_possession#Origins_of_civilian_firearms_possession" title="History of Czech civilian firearms possession">the Hussite revolt</a> in Bohemia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Muskets">Muskets</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">Musket</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Muzzleloader" title="Muzzleloader">Muzzle-loading</a> muskets (smooth-bored long guns) were among the first firearms developed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2018)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> The firearm was loaded through the muzzle with gunpowder, optionally with some wadding, and then with a bullet (usually a solid lead ball, but musketeers could shoot stones when they ran out of bullets). Greatly improved muzzleloaders (usually rifled instead of smooth-bored) are manufactured today and have many enthusiasts, many of whom hunt large and small game with their guns. Muzzleloaders have to be manually reloaded after each shot; a skilled archer could fire multiple arrows faster than most early muskets could be reloaded and fired, although by the mid-18th century when muzzleloaders became the standard small-armament of the military, a well-drilled soldier could fire six rounds in a minute using prepared cartridges in his musket. Before then, the effectiveness of muzzleloaders was hindered both by the low reloading speed and, before the firing mechanism was perfected, by the very high risk posed by the firearm to the person attempting to fire it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>One interesting solution to the reloading problem was the "Roman Candle Gun" with <a href="/wiki/Superposed_load" title="Superposed load">superposed loads</a>. This was a muzzleloader in which multiple charges and balls were loaded one on top of the other, with a small hole in each ball to allow the subsequent charge to be ignited after the one ahead of it was ignited. It was neither a very reliable nor popular firearm, but it enabled a form of "automatic" fire long before the advent of the machine gun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Loading_techniques">Loading techniques</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Muzzleloader" title="Muzzleloader">Muzzleloader</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meyers_b8_s0102a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1649" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Meyers_b8_s0102a.jpg/220px-Meyers_b8_s0102a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Meyers_b8_s0102a.jpg/330px-Meyers_b8_s0102a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Meyers_b8_s0102a.jpg/440px-Meyers_b8_s0102a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meyers_b8_s0102a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Percussion_cap" title="Percussion cap">Percussion cap</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Bolt_action" title="Bolt action">bolt action</a> form</div></div></div>
<p>Most early firearms were muzzle-loading. This form of loading has several disadvantages, such as a slow rate of fire and having to expose oneself to enemy fire to reload—as the weapon had to be pointed upright so the powder could be poured through the muzzle into the breech, followed by the ramming the projectile into the breech. As effective methods of sealing the breech developed along with sturdy, weatherproof, self-contained metallic cartridges, muzzle-loaders were replaced by single-shot breech loaders. Eventually, single-shot weapons were replaced by the following repeater-type weapons.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_magazines">Internal magazines</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Magazine_(firearms)" title="Magazine (firearms)">Magazine (firearms)</a></div>
<p>Many firearms made from the late-19th century through the 1950s used internal magazines to load the cartridge into the chamber of the weapon. The most notable and revolutionary weapons of this period appeared during the U.S. Civil War of 1861–1865: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spencer_rifle" title="Spencer rifle">Spencer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_rifle" title="Henry rifle">Henry</a> repeating rifles. Both used fixed tubular magazines, the former having the magazine in the buttstock and the latter under the barrel, which allowed a larger capacity. Later weapons used fixed box magazines that could not be removed from the weapon without disassembling the weapon itself. Fixed magazines permitted the use of larger cartridges and eliminated the hazard of having the bullet of one cartridge butting next to the primer or rim of another cartridge. These magazines are loaded while they are in the weapon, often using a stripper clip. A clip is used to transfer cartridges into the magazine. Some notable weapons that use internal magazines include the <a href="/wiki/Mosin%E2%80%93Nagant" title="Mosin–Nagant">Mosin–Nagant</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauser_Kar_98k" title="Mauser Kar 98k">Mauser Kar 98k</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Springfield_M1903" title="Springfield M1903">Springfield M1903</a>, the <a href="/wiki/M1_Garand" title="M1 Garand">M1 Garand</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/SKS" title="SKS">SKS</a>. Firearms that have internal magazines are usually, but not always, rifles. Some exceptions to this include the Mauser C96 pistol, which uses an internal magazine, and the <a href="/wiki/Breda_30" title="Breda 30">Breda 30</a>, an Italian light machine gun.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Detachable_magazines">Detachable magazines</span></h4>
<p>Many modern firearms use what are called detachable or box magazines as their method of chambering a cartridge. Detachable magazines can be removed from the weapon without disassembling the firearms, usually by pushing a magazine release.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Belt-fed_weapons">Belt-fed weapons</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Belt_(firearm)" title="Belt (firearm)">Belt (firearm)</a></div>
<p>A belt or ammunition belt, a device used to retain and feed cartridges into a firearm, is commonly used with machine guns. Belts were originally composed of canvas or cloth with pockets spaced evenly to allow the belt to be mechanically fed into the gun. These designs were prone to malfunctions due to the effects of oil and other contaminants altering the belt. Later belt-designs used permanently-connected metal links to retain the cartridges during feeding. These belts were more tolerant to exposure to solvents and oil. Notable weapons that use belts include the M240, the M249, the M134 Minigun, and the PK Machine Gun.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Firing_mechanisms">Firing mechanisms</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Trigger_(firearms)" title="Trigger (firearms)">Trigger (firearms)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firearm_action" title="Firearm action">Firearm action</a></div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Matchlock">Matchlock</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Matchlock" title="Matchlock">Matchlock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edo_period_rifles.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Edo_period_rifles.jpg/220px-Edo_period_rifles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Edo_period_rifles.jpg/330px-Edo_period_rifles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Edo_period_rifles.jpg/440px-Edo_period_rifles.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edo_period_rifles.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various Japanese (samurai) <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> matchlocks (<i>tanegashima</i>)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Matchlock" title="Matchlock">Matchlocks</a> were the first and simplest firearms-firing mechanisms developed. In the matchlock mechanism, the powder in the gun barrel was ignited by a piece of burning cord called a "match". The match was wedged into one end of an S-shaped piece of steel. When the trigger (often actually a lever) was pulled, the match was brought into the open end of a "touch hole" at the base of the gun barrel, which contained a very small quantity of gunpowder, igniting the main charge of gunpowder in the gun barrel. The match usually had to be relit after each firing. The main parts of the matchlock firing mechanism are the pan, match, arm, and trigger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> A benefit of the pan and arm swivel being moved to the side of the gun was it gave a clear line of fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> An advantage to the matchlock firing mechanism is that it did not misfire. However, it also came with some disadvantages. One disadvantage involved weather: in rain, the match could not be kept lit to fire the weapon.  Another issue with the match was it could give away the position of soldiers because of the glow, sound, and smell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> While European pistols were equipped with wheellock and flintlock mechanisms, Asian pistols used<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (February 2022)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> matchlock mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Wheellock">Wheellock</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wheellock" title="Wheellock">Wheellock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheellock_for_a_firearm,_17th_century,_iron_-_Stadtmuseum_Fembohaus_-_Nuremberg,_Germany_-DSC02125.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2154" data-file-width="4680" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Wheellock_for_a_firearm%2C_17th_century%2C_iron_-_Stadtmuseum_Fembohaus_-_Nuremberg%2C_Germany_-DSC02125.jpg/220px-Wheellock_for_a_firearm%2C_17th_century%2C_iron_-_Stadtmuseum_Fembohaus_-_Nuremberg%2C_Germany_-DSC02125.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Wheellock_for_a_firearm%2C_17th_century%2C_iron_-_Stadtmuseum_Fembohaus_-_Nuremberg%2C_Germany_-DSC02125.jpg/330px-Wheellock_for_a_firearm%2C_17th_century%2C_iron_-_Stadtmuseum_Fembohaus_-_Nuremberg%2C_Germany_-DSC02125.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Wheellock_for_a_firearm%2C_17th_century%2C_iron_-_Stadtmuseum_Fembohaus_-_Nuremberg%2C_Germany_-DSC02125.jpg/440px-Wheellock_for_a_firearm%2C_17th_century%2C_iron_-_Stadtmuseum_Fembohaus_-_Nuremberg%2C_Germany_-DSC02125.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wheellock_for_a_firearm,_17th_century,_iron_-_Stadtmuseum_Fembohaus_-_Nuremberg,_Germany_-DSC02125.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wheellock pistol mechanism from the 17th century</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wheellock" title="Wheellock">wheellock</a> action, a successor to the matchlock, predated the flintlock. Despite its many faults, the wheellock was a significant improvement over the matchlock in terms of both convenience and safety, since it eliminated the need to keep a smoldering match in proximity to loose gunpowder. It operated using a small wheel (much like that on a <a href="/wiki/Lighter" title="Lighter">cigarette lighter</a>) which was wound up with a key before use and which, when the trigger was pulled, spun against a flint, creating the shower of sparks that ignited the powder in the touch hole. Supposedly invented by <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> (1452–1519), the Italian <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">Renaissance man</a>, the wheellock action was an innovation that was not widely adopted due to the high cost of the clockwork mechanism.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Flintlock">Flintlock</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flintlock" title="Flintlock">Flintlock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FlintlockMechanism.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="634" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/FlintlockMechanism.jpg/220px-FlintlockMechanism.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/FlintlockMechanism.jpg/330px-FlintlockMechanism.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/FlintlockMechanism.jpg/440px-FlintlockMechanism.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FlintlockMechanism.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flintlock mechanism</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Flintlock" title="Flintlock">flintlock</a> action represented a major innovation in firearm design. The spark used to ignite the gunpowder in the touch hole came from a sharpened piece of flint clamped in the jaws of a "cock" which, when released by the trigger, struck a piece of steel called the "<a href="/wiki/Frizzen" title="Frizzen">frizzen</a>" to generate the necessary sparks. (The spring-loaded arm that holds a piece of flint or pyrite is referred to as a cock because of its resemblance to a rooster.) The cock had to be manually reset after each firing, and the flint had to be replaced periodically due to wear from striking the frizzen. (See also <a href="/wiki/Flintlock_mechanism" title="Flintlock mechanism">flintlock mechanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snaphance" title="Snaphance">snaphance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miquelet_lock" title="Miquelet lock">Miquelet lock</a>.) The flintlock was widely used during the 17th, 18th, and 19th centuries in both muskets and rifles.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Percussion_cap">Percussion cap</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Percussion_cap" title="Percussion cap">Percussion cap</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caplock_mechanism" title="Caplock mechanism">Caplock mechanism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Percussion_cap" title="Percussion cap">Percussion caps</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caplock_mechanism" title="Caplock mechanism">caplock mechanisms</a>), coming into wide service in the early 19th century, offered a dramatic improvement over flintlocks. With the percussion-cap mechanism, the small primer charge of gunpowder used in all preceding firearms was replaced by a completely self-contained explosive charge contained in a small brass "cap". The cap was fastened to the touch hole of the gun (extended to form a "nipple") and ignited by the impact of the gun's "hammer". (The hammer is roughly the same as the cock found on flintlocks except that it does not clamp onto anything.) In the case of percussion caps the hammer was hollow on the end to fit around the cap in order to keep the cap from fragmenting and injuring the shooter.
</p><p>Once struck, the flame from the cap, in turn, ignited the main charge of gunpowder, as with the flintlock, but there was no longer any need to charge the touch hole with gunpowder, and even better, the touch hole was no longer exposed to the elements. As a result, the percussion-cap mechanism was considerably safer, far more weatherproof, and vastly more reliable (cloth-bound cartridges containing a pre-measured charge of gunpowder and a ball had been in regular military service for many years, but the exposed gunpowder in the entry to the touch hole had long been a source of misfires). All <a href="/wiki/Muzzleloader" title="Muzzleloader">muzzleloaders</a> manufactured since the second half of the 19th-century use percussion caps except those built as replicas of the flintlock or earlier firearms.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cartridges">Cartridges</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cartridge_(firearms)" title="Cartridge (firearms)">Cartridge (firearms)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Magazine_(firearms)" title="Magazine (firearms)">Magazine (firearms)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammunition" title="Ammunition">Ammunition</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snider-Martini-Enfield_Cartridges.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="668" data-file-width="967" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Snider-Martini-Enfield_Cartridges.JPG/220px-Snider-Martini-Enfield_Cartridges.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Snider-Martini-Enfield_Cartridges.JPG/330px-Snider-Martini-Enfield_Cartridges.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Snider-Martini-Enfield_Cartridges.JPG/440px-Snider-Martini-Enfield_Cartridges.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snider-Martini-Enfield_Cartridges.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>(From left to right): A <a href="/wiki/.577_Snider" title=".577 Snider">.577 Snider</a> cartridge (1867), a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/.577/450_Martini-Henry" title=".577/450 Martini-Henry">.577/450 Martini-Henry</a> cartridge (1871), a later drawn brass .577/450 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martini-Henry" title="Martini-Henry">Martini-Henry</a> cartridge, and a <a href="/wiki/.303_British" title=".303 British">.303 British</a> Mk VII SAA Ball cartridge.</div></div></div>
<p>Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Louis-Nicolas_Flobert" title="Louis-Nicolas Flobert">Louis-Nicolas Flobert</a> invented the first <a href="/wiki/Rimfire_ammunition" title="Rimfire ammunition">rimfire metallic cartridge</a> in 1845. His cartridge consisted of a percussion cap with a bullet attached to the top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Flobert then made what he called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parlor_gun" title="Parlor gun">parlor guns</a>" for this cartridge, as these rifles and pistols were designed to be shot in indoor shooting-parlors in large homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BarnesBodinson2009_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-BarnesBodinson2009-51">[51]</a></sup> These <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/6mm_Flobert" title="6mm Flobert">6mm Flobert</a> cartridges do not contain any powder, the only <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellant</a> substance contained in the cartridge is the percussion cap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In English-speaking countries, the 6mm Flobert cartridge corresponds to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/.22_BB_Cap" title=".22 BB Cap">.22 BB Cap</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/.22_CB_Cap" title=".22 CB Cap">.22 CB Cap</a> ammunition. These cartridges have a relatively low muzzle-velocity of around 700 ft/s (210 m/s).
</p><p>Cartridges represented a major innovation: firearms ammunition, previously delivered as separate bullets and powder, was combined in a single metallic (usually brass) <a href="/wiki/Cartridge_(firearms)" title="Cartridge (firearms)">cartridge</a> containing a percussion cap, powder, and a bullet in one weatherproof package. The main technical advantage of the brass cartridge case was the effective and reliable sealing of high-pressure gasses at the breech, as the gas pressure forces the cartridge case to expand outward, pressing it firmly against the inside of the gun-barrel chamber.  This prevents the leakage of hot gas which could injure the shooter. The brass cartridge also opened the way for modern repeating arms, by uniting the bullet, gunpowder, and primer into one assembly that could be fed reliably into the breech by mechanical action in the firearm.
</p><p>Before this, a "cartridge" was simply a pre-measured quantity of <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> together with a ball in a small cloth bag (or rolled paper cylinder), which also acted as <a href="/wiki/Wadding" title="Wadding">wadding</a> for the charge and ball. This early form of cartridge had to be rammed into the muzzleloader's barrel, and either a small charge of gunpowder in the touch hole or an external percussion cap mounted on the touch hole ignited the gunpowder in the cartridge. Cartridges with built-in percussion caps (called "primers") continue to this day to be the standard in firearms. In cartridge-firing firearms, a hammer (or a firing pin struck by the hammer) strikes the cartridge primer, which then ignites the gunpowder within. The primer charge is at the base of the cartridge, either within the rim (a <a href="/wiki/Rimfire_ammunition" title="Rimfire ammunition">"rimfire"</a> cartridge) or in a small percussion cap embedded in the center of the base (a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centerfire" title="Centerfire">centerfire</a>" cartridge). As a rule, centerfire cartridges are more powerful than rimfire cartridges, operating at considerably higher pressures than rimfire cartridges. Centerfire cartridges are also safer, as a dropped rimfire cartridge has the potential to discharge if its rim strikes the ground with sufficient force to ignite the primer. This is practically impossible with most centerfire cartridges.
</p><p>Nearly all contemporary firearms load cartridges directly into their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breech-loading_weapon" title="Breech-loading weapon">breech</a>. Some additionally or exclusively load from a <a href="/wiki/Magazine_(firearms)" title="Magazine (firearms)">magazine</a> that holds multiple cartridges. A magazine is defined<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (February 2022)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> as a part of the firearm which exists to store ammunition and to assist in its feeding by the action into the breech (such as through the rotation of a revolver's cylinder or by spring-loaded platforms in most pistol and rifle designs). Some magazines, such as that of most centerfire hunting rifles and all revolvers, are internal to and inseparable from the firearm, and are loaded by using a "clip".  A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clip_(ammunition)" title="Clip (ammunition)">clip</a> (the term often mistakingly refers to a detachable "magazine") is a device that holds the ammunition by the rim of the case and is designed to assist the shooter in reloading the firearm's magazine. Examples include revolver <a href="/wiki/Speedloader" title="Speedloader">speedloaders</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Stripper_clip" title="Stripper clip">stripper clip</a> used to aid loading rifles such as the <a href="/wiki/Lee%E2%80%93Enfield" title="Lee–Enfield">Lee–Enfield</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauser_98" title="Mauser 98">Mauser 98</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/En-bloc_clip#En_bloc" title="En-bloc clip">en-bloc clip</a> used in loading the <a href="/wiki/M1_Garand" title="M1 Garand">M1 Garand</a>. In this sense, "magazines" and "clips", though often used synonymously, refer to different types of devices.
</p>
<h4><span id="Repeating.2C_semi-automatic.2C_and_automatic_firearms"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Repeating,_semi-automatic,_and_automatic_firearms">Repeating, semi-automatic, and automatic firearms</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Single-shot" title="Single-shot">Single-shot</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Repeating_rifle" title="Repeating rifle">Repeating rifle</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Semi-automatic_firearm" title="Semi-automatic firearm">Semi-automatic firearm</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FAMAS_dsc06877.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="2334" decoding="async" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/FAMAS_dsc06877.jpg/220px-FAMAS_dsc06877.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/FAMAS_dsc06877.jpg/330px-FAMAS_dsc06877.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/FAMAS_dsc06877.jpg/440px-FAMAS_dsc06877.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FAMAS_dsc06877.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The French <a href="/wiki/FAMAS" title="FAMAS">FAMAS</a>, example of a <a href="/wiki/Bullpup" title="Bullpup">bullpup</a> rifle</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M4w-att.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="728" data-file-width="1340" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/M4w-att.jpg/220px-M4w-att.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/M4w-att.jpg/330px-M4w-att.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/M4w-att.jpg/440px-M4w-att.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M4w-att.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/M4_carbine" title="M4 carbine">M4 carbine</a>, a modern-day service rifle capable of being fired automatically. It is in service by the U.S. military and has a wide ability for customization.</div></div></div>
<p>Many firearms are "single shot": i.e., each time a cartridge is fired, the operator must manually re-cock the firearm and load another cartridge. The classic single-barreled shotgun offers a good example. A firearm that can load multiple cartridges as the firearm is re-cocked is considered a "repeating firearm" or simply a "repeater". A lever-action rifle, a pump-action shotgun, and most bolt-action rifles are good examples of repeating firearms. A firearm that automatically re-cocks and reloads the next round with each trigger-pull is considered a semi-automatic or autoloading firearm.
</p><p>The first "rapid firing" firearms were usually similar to the 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Gatling_gun" title="Gatling gun">Gatling gun</a>, which would fire cartridges from a magazine as fast as and as long as the operator turned a crank. Eventually, the "rapid" firing mechanism was perfected and miniaturized to the extent that either the recoil of the firearm or the gas pressure from firing could be used to operate it, thus the operator needed only to pull a trigger—this made the firing mechanisms truly "automatic". An <a href="/wiki/Automatic_firearm" title="Automatic firearm">automatic</a> (or "fully automatic") firearm automatically re-cocks, reloads, and fires as long as the trigger is depressed. An automatic firearm is capable of firing multiple rounds with one pull of the trigger. The <a href="/wiki/Gatling_gun" title="Gatling gun">Gatling gun</a> may have been the first automatic weapon, though the modern trigger-actuated machine gun was not widely introduced until the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a> (1914–1918) with the German <a href="/wiki/MG_08" title="MG 08">"Spandau"</a> (adopted in 1908) and the British <a href="/wiki/Lewis_gun" title="Lewis gun">Lewis gun</a> (in service from 1914). Automatic rifles such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Browning_Automatic_Rifle" title="Browning Automatic Rifle">Browning Automatic Rifle</a> were in common use by the military during the early part of the 20th century, and automatic rifles that fired handgun rounds, known as submachine guns, also appeared at this time. Many modern military firearms have a <a href="/wiki/Selective_fire" title="Selective fire">selective fire</a> option, which is a mechanical switch that allows the firearm to be fired either in the semi-automatic or fully automatic mode. In the current M16A2 and M16A4 variants of the U.S.-made <a href="/wiki/M16_rifle" title="M16 rifle">M16</a>, continuous fully-automatic fire is not possible, having been replaced by an automatic burst of three cartridges (this conserves ammunition and increases controllability).
</p><p>Automatic weapons are largely restricted<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (February 2022)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to military and paramilitary organizations, though many automatic designs are infamous for their use by civilians.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_hazards">Health hazards</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gunshot_wound" title="Gunshot wound">Gunshot wound</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gun_safety" title="Gun safety">Gun safety</a></div>
<p>Firearm hazard is quite notable, with a significant impact on the health system. In 2001, for quantification purposes, it was estimated that the cost of fatalities and injuries was US$4700 million per year in Canada (US$170 per Canadian) and US$100,000 million per year in the USA (US$300 per American).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2001_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-who2001-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:327px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2010_homicide_suicide_rates_high-income_countries.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="869" data-file-width="718" decoding="async" height="393" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/2010_homicide_suicide_rates_high-income_countries.png/325px-2010_homicide_suicide_rates_high-income_countries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/2010_homicide_suicide_rates_high-income_countries.png/488px-2010_homicide_suicide_rates_high-income_countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/2010_homicide_suicide_rates_high-income_countries.png/650px-2010_homicide_suicide_rates_high-income_countries.png 2x" width="325"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2010_homicide_suicide_rates_high-income_countries.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gun-related homicide and suicide <i>rates</i> in high-income <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries, 2010, ordered by total death rates (homicide plus suicide plus other gun-related deaths)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJM201603_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJM201603-54">[54]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>From 1990 to 2015, global deaths from assault by firearm rose from 128,000 to 173,000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GDB2013_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-GDB2013-56">[56]</a></sup> however this represents a drop in rate from 2.41/100,000 to 2.35/100,000, as world population has increased by more than two billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPP2015total_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPP2015total-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, there were 39,773 gun-related deaths in the United States; over 60% were suicides from firearms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In 2001, firearms were involved in cases constituting the second leading cause of "mechanism of injury deaths" (which are deaths which occur as a direct, identifiable, and immediate consequence of an event, such as a shooting or poisoning, and do not include deaths due to "natural causes" or "indirect causes" such as chronic alcohol abuse or tobacco use) after <a href="/wiki/Transportation_safety_in_the_United_States" title="Transportation safety in the United States">motor vehicle accidents</a>, which comprised the majority of deaths in this category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The most recent, complete data, from 2017, shows gunshot related homicides as having been the 31st most common cause of death in the US, while gunshot related suicides was the 21st most common cause of death. Accidental discharge of a firearm accounted for the 59th most common cause of death, with 486 deaths in 2017, while 616 individuals were killed by law enforcement, comprising the 58th most common cause of death. The total number of deaths related to firearms in 2017 was 38,882 (not including incidents of deaths resulting from lethal force when used by law enforcement), while the most common cause of death, heart disease, claimed 647,457 lives, over sixteen times that of firearms, including suicides. The most recent data from the CDC, from 2020, shows that deaths involving firearms accounted for about 0.2% of all deaths nationwide in 2020, of which about two-thirds were suicides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 52 high- and middle-income countries, with a combined population of 1,400 million and not engaged in civil conflict, fatalities due to firearm injuries were estimated at 115,000 people per annum, in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2001_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-who2001-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:-moz-fit-content; width:-webkit-fit-content; width:fit-content;"><div class="center noresize" style="width:auto;">
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1039153519">@media all and (max-width:720px){body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .mw-graph{min-width:100%!important;max-width:100%;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:visible}}.mw-parser-output .mw-graph-img{width:inherit;height:inherit}</style><div class="mw-graph mw-graph-always mw-graph-nofallback" data-graph-id="7395517f22e08b528e6589818507c37909d0fdaa" style="min-width:100px;min-height:100px"></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Fatalities in 52 countries, mid-1990s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2001_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-who2001-53">[53]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In those 52 countries, a firearm is the first method used for homicide (two-thirds) but only the second method for suicide (20%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2001_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-who2001-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>To prevent unintentional injury, gun safety training includes education on proper firearm storage and firearm-handling etiquette.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Injury">Injury</span></h3>
<p>Based on US data, it is estimated that three people are injured for one killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2001_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-who2001-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noise">Noise</span></h3>
<p>A common hazard of repeated firearm use is <a href="/wiki/Noise-induced_hearing_loss" title="Noise-induced hearing loss">noise-induced hearing loss</a> (NIHL). NIHL can result from long-term exposure to noise or from high intensity impact noises such as gunshots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-66">[66]</a></sup> Individuals who shoot guns often have a characteristic pattern of hearing loss referred to as "shooters ear". They often have a high-frequency loss with better hearing in the low frequencies and one ear is typically worse than the other. The ear on the side the shooter is holding the gun will receive protection from the sound wave from the shoulder while the other ear remains unprotected and more susceptible to the full impact of the sound wave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The intensity of a gunshot does vary; lower caliber guns are typically on the softer side while higher caliber guns are often louder. The intensity of a gunshot though typically ranges from 140 dB to 175 dB. Indoor shooting also causes loud reverberations which can also be as damaging as the actual gunshot itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup> According to the National Institute on Deafness and Other Communication Disorders, noise above 85 dB can begin to cause hearing loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-65">[65]</a></sup> While many sounds cause damage over time, at the intensity level of a gunshot (140 dB or louder), damage to the ear can occur instantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shooters use custom hearing protection such as electronic type hearing protection for hunters which can amplify soft sounds like leaves crunching while reducing the intensity of the gunshot and custom hearing protection for skeet shooting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even with hearing protection, due to the high intensity of the noise guns produce shooters still develop hearing loss over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_66-4"><a href="#cite_note-:1-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_definitions">Legal definitions</span></h2>
<p>Firearms include a variety of ranged weapons and there is no agreed-upon definition.
For instance,  English language laws of big legal entities such as the United States, India, the European Union and Canada use different definitions.
Other English language definitions are provided by international treaties.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3><p>
In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" title="United States of America">United States</a>, under 26 USC § 5845 (a), the term "firearm" means <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"> <div class="plainlist">
<ul><li>(1)  a shotgun having a  barrel or barrels of less than 18 inches in length;</li>
<li>(2) a weapon made from a shotgun if such weapon as modified has an overall length of less than 26 inches or a barrel or barrels of less than 18 inches in length;</li>
<li>(3) a rifle having a barrel or barrels of less than 16 inches in length;</li>
<li>(4) a weapon made from a rifle if such weapon as modified has an overall length of less than 26 inches or a barrel or barrel of less than 16 inches in length;</li>
<li>(5) any other weapon, as defined in subsection (e);</li>
<li>(6) a machinegun;</li>
<li>(7) any silencer (as defined in section 921 of title 18, United States Code); <br/> The term ‘‘firearm’’ shall not include an antique firearm or any device (other than a machinegun or destructive device) which, although designed as a  weapon, the Secretary finds by reason of the date of its manufacture, value, design, and other characteristics is primarily a collector's item and is not likely to be used as a weapon.</li></ul>
</div></blockquote>
<p>This is the ATF definition of a <a href="/wiki/Title_II_weapons" title="Title II weapons">title II</a> "NFA firearm", as defined by the <a href="/wiki/National_Firearms_Act" title="National Firearms Act">National Firearms Act</a>, and not the definition of a title I firearm, which includes firearms not restricted by the NFA. ATF forms dealing with Title II weapons all state the above-mentioned information, however, the above information is only applicable for the purposes of those forms. For practical purposes, a Firearm, in The US, is defined as the part of a weapon- designed to use expanded gas caused by the combustion of explosive material, to propel a projectile- which houses the fire control group (trigger &amp; sear).
</p><p>According to the US <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Alcohol,_Tobacco,_Firearms_and_Explosives" title="Bureau of Alcohol, Tobacco, Firearms and Explosives">Bureau of Alcohol, Tobacco, Firearms and Explosives</a>, if gas pressurization is achieved through <i>mechanical</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_compression" title="Gas compression">gas compression</a> rather than through chemical propellant combustion, then the device is technically an <a href="/wiki/Air_gun" title="Air gun">air gun</a>, not a firearm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atfairgun_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-atfairgun-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span></h3><p>
In India, the arms act, 1959, provides a definition of firearms where "firearms" means arms of any description designed or adapted to discharge a projectile or projectiles of any kind by the action of any explosive or other forms of energy, and includes:<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li>(i) artillery, hand-grenades, riot-pistols or weapons of any kind designed or adapted for the discharge of any noxious liquid, gas, or other such thing,</li>
<li>(ii) accessories for any such firearm designed or adapted to diminish the noise or flash caused by the firing thereof,</li>
<li>(iii) parts of, and machinery for manufacturing, fire-arms, and</li>
<li>(iv) carriages, platforms, and appliances for mounting, transporting and serving artillery;</li></ul>
</div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span></h3>
<p>In the European Union, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Directive" title="European Directive">European Directive</a> amended by EU directive 2017/853 set minimum standards regarding civilian firearms acquisition and possession that EU Member States must implement into their national legal systems. In this context, since 2017, firearms are considered as "any portable barrelled weapon that expels, is designed to expel or may be converted to expel a shot, bullet or projectile by the action of a combustible propellant".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> 
For legal reasons, objects can be considered a firearm if they have the appearance of a firearm or are made in a way that makes it possible to convert them to a firearm. Member states may be allowed to exclude from their gun control law items such as antique weapons, or specific purposes items that can only be used for that sole purpose.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span></h3>
<p>In the UK a firearm does not have to use a combustible propellant, as explained by <a class="external text" href="https://www.cps.gov.uk/legal-guidance/firearms" rel="nofollow">Crown Prosecution Service Guidance Firearms</a> The Firearms Act 1968 Section 57(1B), uses the definition of a firearm as a "lethal barrelled weapon" as a "barrelled weapon of any description from which a shot, bullet or other missile, with kinetic energy of more than <b>one joule</b> as measured at the muzzle of the weapon, can be discharged". As such, low-energy air rifles and pistols also fall under UK firearm legislation, although the licensing requirements of low-energy weapons are more relaxed.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span></h3>
<p>In Canada, firearms are defined by the Criminal Code:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>firearm means a barrelled weapon from which any shot, bullet, or other projectile can be discharged and that is capable of causing serious bodily injury or death to a person, and includes any frame or receiver of such a barrelled weapon and anything that can be adapted for use as a firearm; (arme à feu)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span></h3>
<p>Australia has a definition of firearms in its 1996 legal act:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"firearm" means any device, whether or not assembled or in parts —
</p><ul><li>(a)     which is designed or adapted, or is capable of being modified, to discharge shot or a bullet or other missile by the expansion of gases produced in the device by the ignition of strongly combustible materials or by compressed air or other gases, whether stored in the device in pressurised containers or produced in the device by mechanical means; and</li>
<li>(b)     whether or not operable or complete or temporarily or permanently inoperable or incomplete</li></ul>
<p>— and which is not —
</p>
<ul><li>(c)     an industrial tool powered by cartridges containing gunpowder or compressed air or other gases which is designed and intended for use for fixing fasteners or plugs or for similar purposes; or</li>
<li>(d)     a captive bolt humane killer; or</li>
<li>(e)     a spear gun designed for underwater use; or</li>
<li>(f)     a device designed for the discharge of signal flares; or</li>
<li>(h)     a device commonly known as a kiln gun or ringblaster, designed specifically for knocking out or down solid material in kilns, furnaces or cement silos; or</li>
<li>(i)     a device commonly known as a line thrower designed for establishing lines between structures or natural features and powered by compressed air to other compressed gases and used for rescue purposes, rescue training or rescue demonstration; or</li>
<li>(j)     a device of a prescribed class;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www5.austlii.edu.au_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-www5.austlii.edu.au-10">[10]</a></sup></li></ul>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">South Africa</span></h3>
<p>In South Africa, Firearms Control Act [No. 60 of 2000] defines firearms since June 2001, with a 2006 amendment of the definition: 
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>'firearm' means any-
</p><ul><li>(a) device manufactured or designed to propel a bullet or projectile through a barrel or cylinder by means of burning propellant, at a muzzle energy exceeding 8 joules (6 ft-lbs);</li>
<li>(b) device manufactured or designed to discharge rim-fire, centre-fire or pin-fire ammunition;</li>
<li>(c) device which is not at the time capable of discharging any bullet or projectile, but which can be readily altered to be a firearm within the meaning of paragraph (a) or (b);</li>
<li>(d) device manufactured to discharge a bullet or any other projectile of a calibre of 5.6 mm (.22 calibre) or higher at a muzzle energy of more than 8 joules (6 ft-lbs), by means of compressed gas and not by means of burning propellant; or [Para. (d) substituted by s. 1 (b) of Act 43 of 2003.]</li>
<li>(e) barrel, frame or receiver of a device referred to in paragraphs (a), (b), (c) or (d), but does not include a muzzle loading firearm or any device contemplated in section 5;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-act60of2000_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-act60of2000-9">[9]</a></sup></li></ul>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_treaties">International treaties</span></h3>
<p>An inter-American convention defines firearms as:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<ul><li>any barreled weapon which will or is designed to or may be readily converted to expel a bullet or projectile by the action of an explosive, except antique firearms manufactured before the 20th Century or their replicas; or</li>
<li>any other weapon or destructive device such as any explosive, incendiary or gas bomb, grenade, rocket, rocket launcher, missile, missile system, or mine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></li></ul>
</blockquote>
<p>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protocol_against_the_Illicit_Manufacturing_of_and_Trafficking_in_Firearms" title="Protocol against the Illicit Manufacturing of and Trafficking in Firearms">international UN protocol on firearms</a> considers that 
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>“Firearm” shall mean any portable barrelled weapon that expels, is designed to expel or may be readily converted to expel a shot, bullet or projectile by the action of an explosive, excluding antique firearms or their replicas. Antique firearms and their replicas shall be defined in accordance with domestic law. In no case, however, shall antique firearms include firearms manufactured after 1899<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p></blockquote>

<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFBooksDickieJesticeJorgensen2009">Books, Amber; Dickie, Iain; Jestice, Phyllis; Jorgensen, Christer; Rice, Rob S.; Dougherty, Martin J.
